179 GRI 403-1 Occupational Health and 
Safety Management System Chapter Empowering People and Communities; Occupational Health and Safety.
403-2 Hazard Identification, 
Risk Assessment, and 
At Banco de Occidente, we carry out a detailed analysis to identify the risks present in all areas of the 
organization, including occupational health and safety issues and environmental care. This process allows 
us to recognize potential threats and apply proactive measures to reduce their impact. Next, we carry out 
a comprehensive risk assessment, where we examine both the probability of occurrence and the possible 
consequences of each identified risk. With this information, we set priorities and design specific strategies 
to manage them effectively. In the event of an incident, we conduct a thorough investigation to identify the 
root causes and implement improvements that optimize our processes and procedures. We focus on avoiding 
the repetition of similar events and continuously strengthening our culture of safety and sustainability.
179 GRI 403-3 Occupational 
Health Services Chapter Empowering People and Communities; Occupational Health and Safety.
## Página 221
Engagement, Consultation, 
and Communication on 
At Banco de Occidente, we are constantly striving to promote safe and healthy practices. The safety and health 
of all employees, regardless of their type of employment relationship with the organization, is a priority for us. 
To ensure the uniform application of these principles throughout our value chain, we have developed robust 
monitoring and evaluation mechanisms. Our employees also actively participate in the Occupational Health 
and Safety Management System (OH&S-MS). In Colombia, we have the COPASST (Joint Committee on Safety 
and Health at Work). This committee is formed in accordance with local regulations, guaranteeing the equitable 
representation of employer and employees, and meets monthly to address occupational safety issues, organize 
training, carry out safety inspections and lead prevention campaigns. Likewise, we continuously monitor key health 
and safety indicators, promoting a constant improvement of working conditions throughout our operation.
179 GRI 403-5 Occupational Health and 
Safety Training of Employees Chapter Empowering People and Communities; Occupational Health and Safety.
179 GRI 403-6 Employees 
From the Occupational Medicine Program, we have implemented a series of initiatives designed to 
promote the comprehensive well-being of our employees, focusing on both physical and mental health 
care. These activities seek to generate healthy habits that contribute to a more balanced life, thus 
improving their quality of life and work performance. Every year we carry out Health Week, an event 
that includes a series of activities focused on the promotion of healthy habits. Activities include:
• Nutrition and healthy eating: workshops and advice on balanced eating.
• Promotion of physical activity: group classes and recommendations for an active life.
• Emotional well-being: talks and workshops on stress management, resilience and mental health.
• Disease prevention: vaccination days and medical check-ups.
• Trends in safety and occupational risks: training in ergonomics and risk prevention.
This event takes place at our administrative headquarters nationwide and is complemented 
by virtual activities to ensure the participation of employees in regional offices.
Recreational and cultural activities: throughout the year, we promote physical activity and emotional well-being through 
various recreational and cultural activities, coordinated by our work environment team. Some of these include:
• Sports tournaments: beach volleyball competitions and corporate marathons.
• Cultural and social activities: ecological walks, film screenings and volunteer 
days to involve employees in social and environmental actions.
In addition, we have established agreements with various sports institutions, such as gyms 
and wellness centers, to offer our employees access to facilities and programs that allow 
them to maintain a regular exercise routine and improve their quality of life.
## Página 222
403-8 Coverage of the 
occupational health and 
safety management system
 Our OSHMS covers all employees, regardless of their work modality, guaranteeing its application in 
all locations. In addition, it includes suppliers and contractors who carry out activities on mission or 
within our facilities, ensuring a safe environment aligned with our risk prevention policies.

## Página 224
Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures
Climate change represents one of the most significant challenges of our time, and at Banco de Occidente 
we are committed to playing an active role in mitigating it. This report, prepared in accordance with the 
recommendations of the Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD), reflects our dedication to 
transparency and climate-related risk management. In this sense, we have developed a sustainability strategy 
that seeks to impact society, protect the environment and promote social development in the territories in 
The TCFD Initiative is a framework of recommendations designed to promote the disclosure of climate-related 
financial information. It seeks to offer solid guidelines that allow companies to communicate more effectively 
the financial risks associated with climate change.
In 2022, we began our roadmap for the adoption of the TCFD framework, also aligning with the instructions 
of the External Circular No. 031 of 2021 of the Finance Superintendence of Colombia (SFC). This process has 
involved different levels and areas of Banco de Occidente, allowing us to address each of the four pillars 
and eleven recommendations of the TCFD framework.
As a financial institution committed to the fight against climate change and in compliance with Colombian 
legislation, we have adhered to this framework, preparing our first TCFD report in 2023. This adhesion 
reinforces our commitment to transparency and management of climate-related financial risks, aligning us 
with international best practices.
At Banco de Occidente, we understand that the fight against climate change requires a collective and 
continuous effort. We are committed to continuing to work to promote sustainable practices and protect 
our planet for future generations.

## Página 226
Alignment with the Sustainability Strategy
With a strong commitment to inclusive economic growth, we work to ensure that our services contribute to 
sustainable development, widespread prosperity and resource conservation, ensuring a positive legacy for 
future generations.
In this line, we adopt principles of Responsible Business Conduct, integrating and addressing environmental 
and social challenges in all our business activities, including our supply chain. Our sustainability strategy is 
based on the principles of the Global Compact and the UN Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), as well 
as on the adoption of the good practices of the Asobancaria Green Protocol. At Banco de Occidente, we are 
dedicated to fostering the development of people, the growth of businesses, and the positive transformation 
of the country through innovative financial solutions.

## Página 228
Climate governance at Banco de Occidente is based on the recognition of the importance of climate change in our operations and in 
our portfolio. We know that as a financial institution we have a great responsibility in managing decarbonization and climate resilience 
throughout our value chain.
In the spirit of advancing these matters, we have held awareness-raising sessions, in collaboration with expert consultants, to train 
our teams on the context of climate change, the recommendations of the TCFD framework, and the guidelines established by External 
Circular No. 031 of 2021. Thus, we seek to impact our Board of Directors, the President, the Support Committees, the Managers, Directors 
and employees of the Bank, including the Steering Committee of Fiduciaria de Occidente.
Climate governancea

three (3) lines of defense:  
First line of defense: Responsible for carrying out 
actions in every process by following the Corporate 
Governance guidelines and the regulations in force 
to prevent materialization of risks.  
Second line of defense: Comprised by the Risk Vice-
Presidency and its managements, responsible for 
evaluating, measuring, monitoring and mitigating 
Third line of defense: Constitutes the Audit area, 
whose purpose is to generate value aligned with 
the Banks’ strategy through the organizational

Environmental and Climate Change Policy of 
Banco de Occidente and Affiliates: In our policy we 
establish the general principles that we must follow 
to align and develop initiatives that respond to the 
commitments of the sustainability strategy, in such a 
way that they are oriented to the management of the 
business around economic, social, environmental and 
corporate governance matters. For us, it is essential 
to consolidate sustainability and its articulation with 
the purpose and business model of the Bank and its 
affiliates. With this policy, we want to strengthen the 
processes for the improvement of business operations, 
organizational reputation and the positioning of the 
organization and its affiliates as a benchmark in the 
Colombian financial sector.

of environmental policy

risk management system for projects financed 
by Banco de Occidente and its financial 
3. P romote financing for eco-efficient projects and

## Página 231
Strategy covering climate change issues
At Banco de Occidente, we have implemented multiple initiatives that allow us to advance in the adoption 
and appropriation of our climate strategy. It has different phases that include the recognition of risks derived 
from climate change, the identification of decarbonization opportunities and the development of products 
and services aligned with these findings. In this way, we contribute to nationally determined goals while 
maintaining the Bank’s carbon neutrality and boosting climate resilience in our clients.

These are those risks related to extreme weather 
events. These can be acute or chronic.
 A cute: Refers to a specific disaster or increase 
in extreme and/or fast-onset weather events 
such as torrential floods, landslides, cyclones, 
floods, and heat waves.
 C hronic: Long-term changes in weather 
patterns, such as changes in precipitation 
regimes and extreme variability in weather 
patterns, increase in the average temperature 
of the region, and rising sea levels.
Distribution of physical 
Distribution of physical risks assessed by time horizon
In this process, Banco de Occidente evaluated 8 physical risks, of which 
62% are acute, i.e., they are unexpected extreme events that could 
impact the Bank’s operations and/or portfolio. Of all the physical risks, 
most are expected to materialize between the short and medium term, 
demonstrating the importance and urgency of acting on mitigation, 
prevention, and adaptation to these risks.

## Página 235
These arise from the political, technological or 
market change associated with the transition to a 
decarbonized economy. Depending on the nature, 
speed, and focus of these changes, transition risks 
can pose risks of varying levels to organizations. 
Within transition risks, the TCFD defines the following 
categories: political and legal risks, technological 
risk, market risk and reputational risk.    
Distribution of the transition 
Distribution of transition risks assessed by time horizon
In this process, the Bank assessed 12 transition risks, of which 62% 
correspond to market, political and legal risks. Unlike what we analyzed 
with the physical risks that have recently materialized in the country 
through fires, water shortages, floods, among others, in the case of 
transition risks, most of these could materialize in the long term.

## Página 239
The stochastic, non-deterministic methodology 
developed in 2023 by Ecoacciones takes into account 
the uncertainty about the future behavior of economic, 
technical, environmental and physical variables. 
This methodology allows assigning probability 
distribution functions to each variable, which enables 
the simulation of the probable distribution in terms of 
frequency and probability, obtaining results in ranges 
instead of point data, strengthening the analysis 
For each risk, the climatic parameters associated with 
its cause or origin are identified, analyzing behaviors 
not only historical but also projected specifically 
for Colombia, taking as a source different climatic 
databases associated with the selected climate 
scenarios. The bases consulted come from models 
and simulations widely accepted by the financial 
industry such as the NGFS network, compilations of 
models from the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate 
Change IPCC on the platform of the International 
Institute for Applied Systems Analysis IIASA; this 
information is interrelated with the Bank’s own specific 
operating variables, which allows us to estimate a 
quantification of the financial value associated with 
each of the risks, either through opportunity costs 
such as lost revenues, or on the other hand to quantify 
impacts such as the potential value of the partial or 
total loss of portfolio due to extreme weather events 
since customers could compromise their ability to 
pay in the face of unforeseen situations.

Climate change management
Recognizing the aforementioned risks and the commitment adopted by the Bank, during 2024 different

## Página 240
offices with the best consumption month 
Portfolio exposure to 
climate risks by sector
As part of the implementation of the TCFD 
recommendations and following the guidance 
provided by the Banking Association of Colombia, 
we have carried out a detailed classification of 
the bank’s loan portfolio according to economic 
sectors, using the International Standard Industrial 
Classification (ISIC). This analysis aims to assess 
and manage the exposure of each sector to climate 
risks, categorizing them according to the nature of 
their activities and their level of vulnerability. 
The sectors have been classified into three categories: 
high, medium and low exposure to climate risk and 
can be seen in the graph below.
This segmentation will allow us to focus efforts on 
those customers who require greater support to 
manage climate risks that may affect their financial 
situation and value generation in the short, medium 
and long term, as well as to promote opportunities that 
can have a positive financial impact on our customers. 
In addition, this segmentation makes it possible to 
accurately identify the areas that require greater 
attention and proactive management, facilitating 
strategic decision-making and the development of 
policies that contribute to the bank’s resilience and 
sustainability in the face of climate challenges. In 
addition to these efforts, during 2024 different stress 
tests were carried out by geography and sector, which 
made it possible to identify some latent risks in the 
portfolio and establish mitigation plans to minimize 
exposure to these climate risks. In this process, it 
was identified that the cost of provisions is higher in 
an adverse scenario with risks derived from climate 
change[1] than in the other scenarios evaluated in 
Criticality distribution according 
to portfolio percentage
In addition, we have identified 14 divisions that 
present a medium climate risk, which constitute 
20% of the portfolio. These divisions, although not 
as exposed as the high-risk still face significant risks 
that must be managed with adequate mitigation 
and adaptation measures.
Finally, 49 sectoral divisions have been classified as 
having low climate risk, representing the remaining 
40% of the portfolio. These divisions have relatively 
lower exposure to climate risks, although it is important 
to continue to monitor their situation to ensure that 
they maintain low exposure and vulnerability to 
potential changes in weather conditions.
However, in order to analyze the trends in our portfolio 
in recent years, we have analyzed the information 
corresponding to 2022 and 2023. In this classification 
of the level of criticality in previous years, the 
percentage of the portfolio exposed to climate risks 
at an average level was 10% lower compared to 2024. 
When analyzing in greater detail the concentration 
of the portfolio by those ISIC divisions classified at 
medium risk, in the graph below, there is evidence 
of an increase between 2022 and 2024 in water 
collection, treatment and distribution activities, 
which includes the operation of irrigation canals, 
the collection, treatment and distribution of water 
for domestic and industrial use, the purification of 
water, among other key activities, which may reflect 
the water crisis in the country, which materialized in 
water rationing in Bogotá. In addition, there are other 
divisions that had lower growth such as Installation, 
maintenance and specialized repair of machinery and 
equipment and Manufacture of other non-metallic 
mineral products, which responds to the recovery 
and gradual growth of the construction sector after 
the COVID 19 pandemic. On the other hand, the 
percentage of the commercial portfolio exposed to 
high climatic risks maintains the proportion in the 
three periods of time with slight variations that may 
respond to market behavior. 
In the detailed analysis of the Bank’s loan portfolio, 
we have identified a total of 24 sector divisions that 
are classified as high climate risk, representing 39% 
of the total portfolio[2]. These divisions are particularly 
vulnerable to the adverse impacts of climate change, 
whether because of the nature of their activities, 
their geographic location, or their dependence on 
climate-sensitive natural resources. 
[1] T he adverse scenario with climate risk takes into account a hypothetical scenario in which the economy and the financial system are 
affected by unexpected shocks and acute physical risks arising from climate change. 
[2] T he remaining 1% of the portfolio corresponds to employees, pensioners and investors. They were not included in the sectoral classification.

## Página 242
A. M anufacture of 
This sector is essential for the country’s food security 
and economic development, but it faces a variety 
of climate risks that can impact its sustainability 
and long-term performance. This category includes 
subsectors such as the production of vegetable and 
animal oils and fats, prepared animal feed, sugar 
and panela, coffee products, milling, dairy products, 
the processing and preservation of meat, fish, fruits 
and vegetables, among others. 
Among the main transition risks we have identified is 
the need to adopt new low-emission technologies, 
which could imply high investments by companies and 
the risk of rapid obsolescence. In addition, changes 
in consumer behavior towards more sustainable 
products and volatility in commodity prices can 
affect the demand and operating costs of each 
company in this sector.
In terms of physical risks, the sector is highly vulnerable 
to extreme weather events such as floods, droughts 
and heat waves, which can disrupt production and 
affect product quality. These events can cause supply 
chain disruptions, increase production costs, and 
reduce the availability of essential inputs. In order 
to review this exposure and generate information to 
improve financial planning and risk management, 
in 2024 we developed different stress tests in which 
different risks such as droughts and rainfall for the 
agricultural sector in the country were reviewed, 
which has made it possible to identify actions to 
mitigate the risks that these climatic events represent 
Legal and reputational risks are also significant, as 
non-compliance with environmental regulations and 
negative public perception can lead to penalties 
and loss of consumer trust. The need to adapt to 
stricter regulations and market expectations for 
sustainability is crucial to staying competitive.
In the macro environment, climate change can alter 
the supply of agricultural products, generating greater 
dependence on imports and affecting the economic 
stability of the sector. The reduction in the quality 
of products and the volatility of hedging financial 
instruments are other factors that can negatively 
impact organizations in this sector.

## Página 245
B. S upply of electricity, gas, 
steam and air conditioning
The electricity, gas, steam and air conditioning supply sector is 
fundamental for development and social welfare in Colombia, as it 
provides essential services that sustain daily life and the functioning 
of the industry. This sector encompasses several critical activities, 
including the generation, transmission, distribution and marketing 
of electricity; gas production and pipeline distribution of gaseous 
fuels; as well as the supply of steam and air conditioning.
Technological risks include falling prices in renewable energy 
generation technology and the costs associated with transitioning to 
lower-emission technologies for conventional fossil fuel generators. In 
Colombia, some generators that rely on fossil fuels must quickly adapt 
to cleaner energy. Technological evolution makes its infrastructure 
obsolete and less competitive, forcing a transition to stay in the 
Based on global trends up to 2024, some market risks have been 
identified in this sector, as the accelerated decline in demand for 
fossil fuels due to decarbonization and the increase in the cost 
of raw materials for electricity generation may pose significant 
threats. The depreciation of different assets due to the pollution 
they generate, together with the fluctuation of energy prices, can 
reconfigure the supply and demand structure of the sector, favoring 
renewable energies. Additionally, in Colombia there is evidence of 
uncertainty regarding the rationing of fundamental resources such 
as water and its possible repercussions on energy supply due to 
the country’s high dependence on hydroelectric plants.  However, 
by 2025 these trends are expected to vary significantly due to 
emerging senior leaders such as Donald Trump in the United States 
who are dissociating themselves from initiatives 
related to decarbonization and the fight against 
Reputational risks include stigmatization of the sector’s 
value chain associated with high emissions and 
negative feedback from investors, forcing changes 
in the business model. Coal and oil production within 
the sector’s value chain will experience a decrease 
in investment flow while consumer preferences, 
influenced by the negative reputation of fossil fuels, 
may reduce their demand.
In terms of physical risks, adverse weather events, 
such as high rainfall, water stress, fires, and heat 
waves, affect costs and investments, reducing 
productivity and efficiency in the fossil energy supply 
chain, and can destroy or disrupt physical assets 
and infrastructure. Chronic risks include rising sea 
levels, which impact cities and coastal territories, and 
the loss of adaptive capacity of ecosystems due to 
prolonged temperature rises. These variations impact 
the frequency and intensity of rainfall, which can alter 
water availability, increase costs and investments, 
and cause disruptions in value chains.

## Página 248
C. Civil engineering works 
and building construction
The building construction and civil engineering sectors 
can be grouped into the same category, as they face 
similar challenges and risks. The civil engineering 
and building construction sector is responsible for 
providing the necessary infrastructure that supports 
daily life and the operation of the industry. This sector 
encompasses several critical activities, including the 
construction of roads and railways, the construction 
of public service projects, and the construction of 
buildings, among others.
Among the transition risks, the need to adopt low-
emission technologies in procedures, which represents 
a significant challenge due to high investments and 
the possibility that such technologies will quickly 
become obsolete. The substitution of current products 
and services with less emissions-intensive options, 
together with the high cost of this transition, are 
considerable threats. In addition, consumers and 
customers are increasingly inclined to prefer projects 
and buildings with a lower carbon footprint, which 
may influence the demand for projects that do not 
integrate sustainable and energy-efficient practices.
Changing consumer behavior can also lead to 
a decrease in demand for products with high 
greenhouse gas emissions. Infrastructure that is not 
adapted to the new climate conditions will face higher 
insurance costs, and carbon-intensive subsectors 
will struggle to obtain financing, which will affect 
their competitiveness.
Eventually, the sector may have to deal with significant 
reputational risks, since according to the World 
Green Building Council (WGBC), the construction 
sector worldwide consumes 50% of the resources 
extracted, uses 15% of the fresh water consumed 
in the world and is responsible for more than 35% 
of CO2 emissions.  That is, their contribution to 
climate change is high ( IDB, 2024 ). This has led 
internationally and may lead to stigmatization of the 
sector in Colombia associated with high emissions 
and negative feedback from investors, which can 
make it difficult to attract financing. In addition, the 
fall in demand for unsustainable constructions could 
harm the competitiveness of companies in this field.
On the other hand, the sector faces both acute and 
chronic physical risks. High rainfall can cause floods 
and avalanches, while low rainfall leads to water stress. 
These adverse weather events will transform supply 
and demand, require infrastructure reconstruction, 
and increase construction and insurance costs. In the 
long term, sea level rise will impact cities and coastal 
territories, affecting productivity and efficiency in 
construction projects.
Infrastructure that is not adapted 
to the new climate conditions 
will face higher insurance 
costs, and carbon-intensive 
subsectors will struggle to obtain 
financing, which will affect their

## Página 251
D. L and transport and 
pipeline transport
The land transport and pipeline transport sector 
is essential for the connectivity and economic 
development of different geographies of the 
country, facilitating the movement of people and 
goods along regions and borders. This sector 
encompasses several key activities, including rail 
transport, pipeline transport, and automotive public 
land transport. Through these modalities, efficient 
mobility, resource distribution and accessibility are 
ensured, contributing significantly to growth and 
socio-economic integration. This sector is particularly 
sensitive to challenges related to the transition to 
more sustainable and low-carbon technologies.
The sector must adapt to increasing pressure 
to reduce carbon emissions. The decrease in 
costs in the production of electric, hydrogen and 
biofuel transports is facilitating this transition for 
the land transport sector, although the lack of 
electric charging infrastructure and the absence 
of incentives for innovation represent significant 
obstacles. If major players do not adopt low-carbon 
technologies, they risk losing regional and local 
competitiveness. Changes in demand favor the use 
of less polluting vehicles and machinery, which can 
lead to depreciation of assets associated with high 
emissions, increase the operating costs of production 
and maintenance of assets, and hinder the distribution 
of the final product due to the persistence in the use 
of obsolete technologies.
In terms of market risks, public perception and 
consumer pressure are leading to a shift in 
preferences towards more sustainable transport 
options. Companies that do not align with these 
changes may face difficulties in attracting finance 
and suffer a drop in demand for carbon-intensive 
transport. This can result in increased price volatility 
and increased capital costs for companies with a 
negative reputation in terms of carbon emissions.
The sector also faces both acute and chronic physical 
risks due to climate change. High rainfall can cause 
flooding and landslides, while low rainfall can lead to 
water stress and increase the frequency of fires. These 
adverse weather events can increase transportation 
costs and require new routes, affecting the efficiency 
of supply chains. In the long term, rising sea levels 
and extreme temperatures can affect infrastructure 
and increase maintenance and operating costs. 
Within the framework of the stress tests, the variations 
in precipitation in this sector were evaluated, for 
which the highest average impact was identified, 
compared to other sectors such as agriculture, real 
estate, commerce, industries, among others. 
The sector must adapt to 
increasing pressure to reduce 
carbon emissions. The decrease 
in costs in the production of 
electric, hydrogen and biofuel 
transports is facilitating this 
transition for the land transport

Potential financial products and services derived from climate change
Opportunities arising from climate change
With the aim of moving forward with the identification of opportunities arising from climate change, in 2023 
we led a comprehensive analysis that included the identification of best market practices, the mapping of 
existing initiatives in the Bank, and the definition of a roadmap that will allow us to advance in decarbonization 
and climate resilience in our operations and portfolio. In this sense, the main opportunities that allow the 
control and mitigation of the organization’s financial risks are:
Decarbonization and Resilience Roadmap Opportunities
Some of these actions have been explored through the opportunity evaluation process and have materialized 
through Banco de Occidente’s sustainable financing, which can be found in the following subchapters of the 
Financing that promotes the adoption of renewable 
energies and energy efficiency 
Financial initiatives that promote the resilience of these 
customers before extreme climate events

regenerative agriculture

Sustainable financing
With the aim of taking advantage of the different opportunities 
arising from climate change, mitigating the financial risks 
we face, and accompanying our clients in the transition to 
decarbonization, during 2024 we developed our Sustainable 
and transition Taxonomy, based on Colombia’s Green Taxonomy 
and the taxonomy designed by IDB Invest. It has 9 eligibility 
categories that are financed under our credit modalities and 
cover issues such as energy, waste management, agriculture, 
mobility, construction, eco-efficiency, nature conservation, 
among others. These categories include measures from 
mitigation to adaptation and have previously established 
indicators and calculation methodologies for their adequate 
monitoring. During 2025, it is expected to formalize and 
disseminate this Taxonomy with the different stakeholders 
to generate transparency in these matters.

Detail of Banco de Occidente’s Sustainable and Transition Taxonomy
Resilience of the climate 
Recognizing the variability of information around climate change and its 
possible impacts, Banco de Occidente has sought to implement different 
initiatives that allow us to guarantee our resilience:
A. R egularly update and revise the assessment of risks related to 
climate change using the prioritized scenarios.
B. A nalyze and assess portfolio exposure by sector and geography to 
prioritize the segments that are most vulnerable to climate change.
C. R egularly analyze the opportunities arising from climate change 
D. S ocialize the different risks and opportunities with the areas in 
charge to promote knowledge within the Bank and generate greater 
E. Coordinate awareness and training spaces in key areas.
F. S eek advice from teams of experts on these issues to review action 
plans and internal initiatives.
G. R espond to the most material risks through measures to avoid, 
prevent or mitigate.
These actions have allowed us to generate the necessary internal 
capacities to guarantee the resilience of our climate change strategy 
in the face of the different impacts or risks that may materialize. 
Eligibility Criteria Description Short-Term 
Automation and control of electrical and mechanical systems.
Heat recovery for hot water (from air conditioning systems, heat or refrigeration pumps, air compressors).
Upgrades of air conditioning and refrigeration equipment by introducing 
refrigerants with very low global warming potential - GWP .
Projects for the use of biofuels or biogas.
Companies that design, sell, and install renewable energy solutions (excluding consumer finance).
Wind energy (residential, commercial and industrial) in distributed applications 
(no grid supply), direct mechanical applications or grid supply.
Green buildings, including housing and social housing and commercial buildings, 
certified under the eligible sustainability certifications LEED Gold or higher or LEED 
for Neighborhood, Casa Colombia or other associated certification.
Aqueduct systems: New integral systems or improvements to existing systems of 
collection, adduction, treatment, storage, conduction and distribution.
Systems and technologies that generate a reduction of at least 20% in annual water consumption
Initiatives for the collection and cleaning of recovered materials, initiatives for returnable packaging systems.
Design and implement post-disaster reconstruction recovery plans with climate change considerations.
Heat chains that support small producers and local agriculture.
Public or personal hybrid or electric vehicles, vehicles that run on vehicular natural gas.
Inter-municipal freight vehicles with zero direct emissions
Construction and maintenance of transport projects with zero direct emissions including their 
key components and supporting infrastructure on subways, buses, trams, trains etc.
Good livestock practices such as livestock feed management, animal selection 
and improvement, breeding to improve animal production.
Sustainable agriculture: agricultural inputs including seeds and fertilizers, sustainable 
agricultural production focused on the protection of ecosystems.
Development of sustainable forest management practices such as seed tree 
protection, census and mapping of commercial trees, forest protection.
Protection and restoration of terrestrial and freshwater ecosystems, biodiversity, habitats and soil.

Integration of processes to manage climate risks and 
opportunities into comprehensive risk management
Based on the relevance of climate risks for Banco de Occidente and the vulnerability of our portfolio, we 
have sought to align this exercise with the organization’s risk culture. This process is documented in our Risk 
Appetite Framework and consists of:  
Comprehensive risk management
Based on this management, we have involved the 
Vice Presidency of Risk and Collection in the different 
stages of identification and measurement of risks 
related to climate change. In addition, several days 
have been held with the teams in charge to socialize 
the TCFD framework and raise awareness among 
the teams about the related knowledge. 
In our risk management, the risk identification stage 
is where we have presented the highest level of 
alignment and where much of our efforts have been 
focused. During 2025 we will continue to work to 
articulate the measurement of risks, the definition 
of appetite and the limits since it is a process that is 
constantly iterating and that is expected to continue 
maturing in the coming months, as we continue to 
accompany our clients in the management of climate 
change and with the continuous monitoring of the 
established climate indicators.
In line with the appetite and limits definition stage, the 
last two stages of comprehensive risk management 
will continue to be strengthened and increasingly 
adjusted, so that the actions taken on climate are 
aligned with the defined climate strategy and fully 
articulated with the bank’s business model.
1.  I dentification of risks: mapping the climate risks related to 
the business plan by contemplating both the qualitative and 
 Measurement of risks: measuring each of the risks under stress 
3. Definition of appetite and caps: define the entity’s risk appetite 
and operational caps. 
4. Strategic and business alignment: developing a strategic plan 
that includes projections, risk appetite, strategic objectives, 
 Monitoring and deviation analysis: monitor the appetite, the 
strategic plan and business strategy.

risks, we developed 4 fundamental steps:

involved in these matters.
D. A lign the risks and opportunities identified 
with the classification proposed by the 
2. Ass ess and prioritize risks and opportunities 
based on chosen criteria:
A. Prioritize climate scenarios to make the 
B.  Assign parameters for each of the climate 
risks and their associated financial 
 P arameter: is an external, scenario-
driven data point that indicates climate 
financial risk. For this case, the database 
of the “NGFS Scenario Explorer Hosted 
by IIASA release 2.2” and the “Climate

 F inancial impact: the bank’s internal 
financial metric that will be affected by 
C. R ate each risk according to the selected 
metrics based on the chosen climate 
 C onfidence level: qualitative rating of the 
reliability of the source and the quality 
 S cenario sensitivity: A measure of the 
divergence of the parameters in the two 
scenarios (greater divergence means a 
less certain risk trend).
 P robability of parameter change: rate of 
change of the parameter (a higher rate 
of change suggests a higher probability).
 T ime horizon: The timeframe in which 
financial impacts reach a predetermined 
D. Prioritize risks under methodologies for 
assigning weights relative to each of the 
metrics by a panel of expert consultants 
and subjective prioritization according 
to the appropriation of knowledge on 
climate change and the operation of the 
bank, where a hierarchical organization is 
carried out. The result of this prioritization 
is the integration of both methodologies 
in a “Materiality vs Certainty” scheme, 
where the valuations of the metrics are 
 M ateriality: Simple average of the “time 
horizon” metric averaged in each climate 
scenario, for a specific parameter/
variable. If there are several parameters 
associated with the same risk, materiality 
will be the result of the simple average 
of the “time horizon” metrics averaged 
for each parameter.
 C ertainty: Simple average of the metrics 
“confidence level”, “probability” and 
“sensitivity”, averaged in each climate 
associated with the same risk, the 
Certainty will be the result of the simple 
average of the metrics “confidence level”, 
“probability” and “sensitivity” averaged 
3. Q uantify the financial value for prioritized risks

developed by Ecoacciones.
B. I dentify the climatic parameters 
associated with their cause or origin, 
analyzing not only historical but also 
projected behaviors.

Process of identification and assessment of 
risks arising from climate change
To develop the analysis of climate-related financial risks, we selected 3 Climate Scenarios Network for 
Greening the Financial System (NGFS) that allow us to understand the implications of market, regulatory, 
technological decisions and the aggressiveness or magnitude of extreme weather events.  
NGFS Climate Scenarios
In order to prioritize and assess the financial risks 
derived from climate change, at Banco de Occidente 
we prioritize three (3) scenarios proposed by NGFS:
 O rderly transition: It is also known as the Net 
Zero 2050 scenario. This scenario contemplates 
high transition risks due to the implementation of 
rigorous measures and regulations immediately 
and low physical risks because emissions and 
impacts on the environment are limited. This 
scenario foresees a temperature increase 
below 1.5°C by 2100. Although it is estimated 
that the world has already surpassed the 
aforementioned temperature increase, this is 
a scenario that guides best business practices 
to avoid, reduce and mitigate the impact on 
 Di sorderly transition: assumes that global 
emissions do not decrease until 2030, rigorous 
policies begin to be implemented from that year 
and there is a large difference in policies and 
ambitions by country, which hinders progress 
at the global level to combat climate change. In 
this scenario, the global temperature increase 
is limited to 1.8°C by 2100.
 H ot house world: it is assumed that the different 
countries maintain the policies that have been 
implemented so far, which implies high physical 
risks and low transition risks. An increase in 
global warming of +3°C is projected by 2080.
Source: NGFS (2025) and WEF (2023)

that guarantee the resilience of Banco de Occidente’s

derived from climate change.  
Since 2023 we have developed the Environmental and 
Social Risk Management System (SARAS) to address 
sustainability issues in our lending operations. It has 
a set of policies, guidelines, tools, and processes that 
allow the identification, evaluation, mitigation, and 
monitoring of the environmental and social risks of 
a project or economic activity. 
By 2025 we will be integrating climate change criteria 
into SARAS, with the aim of addressing these latent 
risks in a phased manner for the most priority sectors 
such as: civil engineering works and construction of 
buildings, production of food products and supply 
of electricity, gas, steam and air conditioning,

## Página 261
GHG reduction target
A Starting in 2022, in relation to our environmental 
commitments, we set a target year and committed 
to reducing our scope 1 and 2 emissions by 4.7% 
per year on a linear basis until 2030. This target will 
allow us to reduce GHG emissions by 42% by 2030 
and offset the remaining 58% through forestry and 
energy programs in the carbon market.
In order to monitor the progress and challenges against 
the commitments we have at Banco de Occidente, we 
have established processes, procedures, and internal 
teams in charge of managing different indicators 
that allow us to move towards the goals we have 
set for ourselves.
As part of our environmental commitments, in 2022 
we reviewed the Science Based Targets initiative 
(SBTi) methodology to chart the trajectory of our 
Greenhouse Gas (GHG) emissions in scopes 1, 2 and 
3. In scope 3, we include financed emissions and 
carry out an analysis of the exposure of our portfolio 
to different climate scenarios. 
As part of our main actions to reduce our GHG 
footprint, we implemented responsible practices 
in the use of resources such as energy, paper and 
water. One of our priority focuses has been the 
energy efficiency project in our offices, as well as 
the sustainable construction and remodeling of our 
facilities. The approved activities for the reduction 
of energy and fuel consumption were evaluated 
in 2023 and began to be implemented in 2024. In 
addition, we have adopted measures focused on the 
management and reduction of waste generation, 
ensuring its correct disposal.
In addition, we have set a final goal of achieving 
carbon neutrality by 2050, both in our direct and 
indirect emissions. To achieve this, we plan to offset 
our emissions through green restoration programs, 
establish more energy efficiency initiatives, and 
other initiatives to be implemented with recognized 
entities with expertise in the field. These programs 
not only include offsetting emissions, but also the 
adoption of clean technologies and the promotion 
of sustainable practices throughout our operations.
Calculation methodology
The measurement of our footprint adheres to and 
fully meets the requirements of the Green House Gas 
Protocol (GHG Protocol). This metric has a focus 
on consolidating operational control and, due to 
the emission sources identified in our operational 
processes, CO2, CH4, N2O, SF6, HCFs and HCFCs 
are included in the calculation.
## Página 262
Emissions intensity by scope 1, 2 and 3 annually
We achieved a reduction in our scope 1 emissions, 
mainly due to the decrease in fuel consumption 
from mobile sources, the reduction of refrigerant 
gases from air conditioners and the change from 
HCFC 123 fire extinguishers to CO2 extinguishers. 
We also continued with preventive maintenance 
of air conditioners, which contributes to reducing 
fugitive emissions.
Despite the decrease in energy consumption at our 
facilities during 2023 and 2024, the emission factor 
provided by XM for the National Interconnected 
System had a variation of 53.76% for 2023 compared 
to 2022 and 22.86% for 2024 compared to 2022, 
which affected our scope 2 emissions. This increase 
is mainly attributed to the effects of the El Niño 
phenomenon, which reduced water inputs during 
the rest of the year, leading to an increase in thermal 
generation to meet national energy demand. 
We managed to significantly reduce our scope 3 
emissions, mainly due to the decrease in corporate 
flights. To achieve this, we intensified the dissemination 
and reminder of our travel expense policy, working 
closely with all areas to efficiently manage the budget 
and reduce the number of trips made.
In addition, paper consumption, waste generation, 
purchases of goods and transportation of employees 
to and from the Bank were reduced. The latter aspect 
has been supported by both the hybrid work scheme 
and the sustainable mobility program.
Overall, our total emissions showed a reduction of 
18.19% for 2023 and 4.83% for 2024 as a result of 
the initiatives and actions undertaken during these 
Reduction of annual emissions

 Ge neral trainings: Informative sessions

## Página 265
Recognizing the importance of aligning understanding, processes and guidelines at Banco de 
Occidente, we are in the process of reviewing some initiatives that will allow us to strengthen 
our approach and management of climate change.  
A. Risks arising from climate change: 
Taking into account that climatic conditions, predictions and geographical realities vary to 
the extent that there is more research, knowledge and experience, we have undertaken some 
internal measures that allow us to strengthen, align and institutionalize internal processes to 
integrate the analysis of risks derived from climate change into the company’s comprehensive 
management of strategic risks. In addition, we will periodically update the risk and opportunity 
mapping, including detailed analyses of the sectors that make up the portfolio, their geography 
and the possible impact on our operations. In this sense, we seek to establish the appropriate 
methodologies to collect and process information based on international guidelines and 
guides to incorporate the best practices in the market.  
B. Assessment of the impacts of climate change:
We are training our teams and strengthening internal capacities to quantitatively assess the 
actual or potential impacts that arise as a result of the materialization of climate change-
related risks. These processes will contemplate different time horizons and can be segmented 
by location, economic sector, among other details that will be useful to make more informed 
C. Strengthening the Climate Strategy:
We will continue to socialize and strengthen our Climate Strategy so that Banco de Occidente’s 
employees fully understand the action fronts, commitments, actions and measures that 
are being taken within the framework of this corporate guideline. Likewise, we will seek to 
incorporate the appropriate initiatives that allow the Strategy to adapt to the changing 
realities we face as a society.  
In order to continue incorporating climate risk measurement into 
Banco de Occidente’s overall operations, we are exploring different 
measures that permeate processes and procedures that have been 
adopted and appropriated by the teams in charge.  
Define appropriate internal 
frameworks and methodologies:
In the course of 2025, we hope to move forward with the creation of 
an annex to the general risk model that specifies the assessment, 
methodologies, and guidelines for climate risks. We will also be 
evaluating the relevance of classifying climate risk as a strategic risk 
at the corporate level along with the definition of the Bank’s appetite 
and its operational limits.
Complementing the aforementioned exercises, expert consulting will 
deepen the strengthening of the comprehensive management of climate 
change while integrating climate risks into the organization’s SARAS. 
In addition, we will work on the construction and implementation of 
a strategic plan that allows us to understand, advance and mitigate 
the prioritized risks with metrics and KPIs that enable the monitoring 
and supervision of climate risks.

B. Process of defining metrics and objectives
A significant part of the risks identified correspond to climate risks that can be transformed into credit risks. 
Recognizing that climate change requires an iterative approach, the Bank has decided to take its first steps 
to address and manage the following risks: The bank’s lack of alignment with the sustainable preferences of 
its customers and the increase in the bank’s costs due to the incorporation of low-carbon technologies. To 
address these challenges, the following metrics and objectives have been established:  
## Página 267
Objectives of the Climate Strategy:
 Minim um annual reduction of 4.7% of scope 1 and 2 GHG emissions annually.
 T otal reduction of 42% of scope 1 and 2 GHG emissions by 2030.
 A chieve carbon neutrality by 2050.

 R educe energy consumption in the Bank’s facilities by 20%.
 R educe water use in the Bank’s facilities by 10%.
 U se 100% renewable energy sources for the Bank’s facilities.
 R educe waste generation in the Bank’s facilities by 25%.
 R educe the use of paper in the Bank’s facilities by 25%.

the portfolio, progress is being made in the development 
of opportunities through our credit lines. This allows us to 
accompany, advise and encourage clients in their transition to 
more sustainable and climate-resilient practices. Additionally, 
with regard to portfolio management, we will continue to 
deepen the analysis of sectoral exposure and by geography.
